        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.9b10312"><meta property="og:title" content="In Situ FTIR-Assisted Synthesis of Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Cathodes for Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/acsami.9b10312.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Prussian blue analogs (PBAs) with stable framework structures are ideal cathodes for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries. The chelating agent-assisted coprecipitate method is an effective way to obtain low-defect PBAs that can limit the appearance of too many vacancies and water molecules and achieve an optimized Na-storage performance. However, for this method, the mechanism of chelating agent-assisted synthesis is still unclear. Herein, the synthesis process of nickel hexacyanoferrate (NiHCF) has been investigated by in situ infrared spectroscopy detection. The results show that the chelating agent oxalate slows down the nucleation process and effectively inhibits the formation of the Fe–C≡N–Ni frame in the aging process, producing highly crystallized and low-defect NiHCF samples. High-quality NiHCF presents a high specific capacity of 86.3 mAh g–1 (a theoretical value of ∼85 mAh g–1), an ultrastable cyclic retention of 90% over 800 cycles, and a remarkable high capacity retention of 74.6% at a current density of 4250 mA g–1 (50C). Particularly, the NiHCF//hard carbon full cell presents a high specific energy density of over 210 Wh kg–1 and an outstanding cyclic stability without obvious capacity attenuation over 1000 cycles."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/acsami.9b10312.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93188a180b2354b8-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188a180b2354b8-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '9ed8774c-da87-4040-b886-f36198235018'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93188a180b2354b8-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="93188a180b2354b8-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ FTIR-Assisted Synthesis of Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Cathodes for Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b10312", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ FTIR-Assisted Synthesis of Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Cathodes for Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b10312", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ FTIR-Assisted Synthesis of Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Cathodes for Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b10312", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ FTIR-Assisted Synthesis of Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Cathodes for Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b10312", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ FTIR-Assisted Synthesis of Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Cathodes for Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b10312", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ FTIR-Assisted Synthesis of Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Cathodes for Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b10312", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ FTIR-Assisted Synthesis of Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Cathodes for Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b10312", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ FTIR-Assisted Synthesis of Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Cathodes for Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b10312", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "In Situ FTIR-Assisted Synthesis of Nickel Hexacyanoferrate Cathodes for Long-Life Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.9b10312", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/medium/am9b10312_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b10312&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Prussian blue analogs (PBAs) with stable framework structures are ideal cathodes for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries. The chelating agent-assisted coprecipitate method is an effective way to obtain low-defect PBAs that can limit the appearance of too many vacancies and water molecules and achieve an optimized Na-storage performance. However, for this method, the mechanism of chelating agent-assisted synthesis is still unclear. Herein, the synthesis process of nickel hexacyanoferrate (NiHCF) has been investigated by in situ infrared spectroscopy detection. The results show that the chelating agent oxalate slows down the nucleation process and effectively inhibits the formation of the Fe–C≡N–Ni frame in the aging process, producing highly crystallized and low-defect NiHCF samples. High-quality NiHCF presents a high specific capacity of 86.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (a theoretical value of ∼85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), an ultrastable cyclic retention of 90% over 800 cycles, and a remarkable high capacity retention of 74.6% at a current density of 4250 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (50C). Particularly, the NiHCF//hard carbon full cell presents a high specific energy density of over 210 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> and an outstanding cyclic stability without obvious capacity attenuation over 1000 cycles.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D29985%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYue%2BXu%252C%2BMiao%2BChang%252C%2BChun%2BFang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D11%26issueNum%3D33%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b10312%26title%3DIn%2BSitu%2BFTIR-Assisted%2BSynthesis%2Bof%2BNickel%2BHexacyanoferrate%2BCathodes%2Bfor%2BLong-Life%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D29992%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2019" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292290&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Crystallization"> Crystallization </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells"> Electrochemical cells </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions"> Ions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291675&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Stability"> Stability </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Prussian+Blue+Analog+Cathode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Prussian%20blue%20analog%20cathode">Prussian blue analog cathode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=In+Situ+FTIR+Studies&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_in%20situ%20FTIR%20studies">in situ FTIR studies</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Long+Cycle+Life&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_long%20cycle%20life">long cycle life</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), as appropriate energy storage systems for large-scale applications, have gained a lot of attention. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Prussian blue analogs (PBAs), open-framework hosts for Na<sup>+</sup> ions, are attractive candidates as SIB cathode materials due to their low cost, environmentally friendly synthesis procedure, wide migration channel, and a large number of Na<sup>+</sup>-occupancy sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 4 to 8">(4−8)</a> Typically, PBAs are represented by the formula A<i><sub>x</sub></i>M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<i><sub>y</sub></i>·β<sub>1–<i>y</i></sub>·<i>n</i>H<sub>2</sub>O, where symbol A represents the alkaline metal ion, M the transition metal ion, and β [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> However, electrochemical active-site Fe reduced with the increasing amount of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies, resulting in the decrease of specific capacities. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> The vacancies can be occupied by water molecules, leading to fragile frameworks that will likely collapse during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process, which results in poor cyclic performances. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12,13)</a> It is reported that the chelating agent-assisted coprecipitate method is effective in adjusting the synthesis process and yields highly crystallized PBAs with minimal defects. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> The controlled synthesis PBA products often present enhanced electrochemical performances, such as high specific capacity, high rate, and long-life stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> With the variation of transition metals (M in A<i><sub>x</sub></i>M[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<i><sub>y</sub></i>·β<sub>1–<i>y</i></sub>·<i>n</i>H<sub>2</sub>O), the complexation stability constant of the chelating agent changes and the modulation mechanisms on PBA nucleation and growth also change. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 15 and 16">(15,16)</a> Appropriate chelating agents are required to match various transition metals and obtain highly crystallized, low-vacancy PBAs. At present, the direct identification of activation in chelating agent-assisted synthesis remains challenging.</div><div class="NLM_p">One of the specific characteristics of PBAs is the presence of cyanide (C≡N) linkers, which can be identified by infrared spectroscopy measurements. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 17 and 18">(17,18)</a> The cyanide stretching modes are closely related to their local environment, including the oxidation state of the center Fe ions, different kinds of transition metals, and the change in the local symmetry of the framework caused by Na<sup>+</sup> interactions. Recently, in situ infrared spectroscopy was used to directly identify and quantitatively describe the catalytically active sites by operando probing of their interfacial bonding environment. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Infrared spectra are sensitive to the coordinate environment of the chemical bond. In the PBA synthesis process, when the Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>3–/4–</sup> group coordinates with the transition metal, the charge density at the N end shifts towards the transition metal, the C≡N binding energy shifts to higher values, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> and the C≡N group vibration band should shift to higher wavenumbers in infrared spectroscopy. According to the sensitivity of C≡N to its bonding environment, in situ Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy characterization is an effective way to monitor the formation process of PBA frameworks.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> as a chelating agent assists the coprecipitate synthesis of nickel hexacyanoferrate (NiHCF). According to the different ratios of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to NiCl<sub>2</sub> (C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup>/Ni = 1, 2, 3), the stability constants of the coordination compounds varied. Then, NiHCF products synthesized under different control conditions were obtained. Operando detection with in situ FTIR was performed to analyze the reaction mechanisms of the addition of oxalate agents. The C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup>-controlled synthesis of NiHCF presents superior Na<sup>+</sup>-storage performances. In particular, NiHCF-3 exhibits a specific capacity of 86.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, an excellent rate capability as maintenance capacity of 64.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 50C, and ultrastable characteristics with the capacity retention of 90% over 800 redox cycles. Full cells were fabricated with a NiHCF-3 cathode and hard carbon anode. The NiHCF-HC full cells present competitive specific energy of over 210 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> and noticeable, long-cyclic performance, as no obvious capacity fading occurs over 1000 cycles.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p">NiHCF samples were prepared by a coprecipitation route assisted with Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to control the crystallization process. NiCl<sub>2</sub> (5 mmol) and an appropriate amount of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> were added into 200 mL of deionized (DI) water and stirred homogeneously to form solution A. Solution B was formed by adding 5 mmol Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>·10H<sub>2</sub>O, 10 mmol NaCl, and 1.5 g of PVP-K30 into 200 mL of deionized water. After being dissolved uniformly, a peristaltic pump was used to add dropwise solution A into solution B, continuously for 12 h. After 24 h of aging, the precipitated NiHCF particles were filtered and washed several times with ethanol and DI water. Finally, the particles were vacuum-dried at 80 °C for over 12 h. According to the ratio of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to NiCl<sub>2</sub> (C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup>/Ni = 0, 1, 2, 3), products were marked as NiHCF-0, NiHCF-1, NiHCF-2, and NiHCF-3, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The hard carbon (HC) anode was synthesized by the hydrothermal and carbonization routes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> First, appropriate amounts of formaldehyde, resorcinol, and aqueous ammonia were mixed in a beaker under vigorous stirring for 20 h. Then, a spherical polymer precursor was prepared by a hydrothermal reaction at 100 °C for 30 h. The precursor was filtered and washed three times with ethanol and DI water, respectively. Subsequently, the sample was vacuum-dried at 80 °C for 48 h. Finally, HC products were obtained after carbonization at a processing temperature of 1200 °C for 2 h under an N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">In situ FTIR characterizations were performed to identify the nucleation and growth process on Fourier transform infrared instruments (two L160000A FTIR spectrometers). To characterize the reaction process constantly, a VeeMAX III ATR in situ reaction tank was attached to the infrared spectrometer. Solution B, containing Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, was first added into the reaction tank, and the initial C≡N band was detected at wavenumbers between 1800 and 2300 cm<sup>–1</sup>. Upon mixing solutions A and B in the reaction tank, the FTIR data were collected continuously for every 20 s in the first minute and then collected once a minute.</div><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray diffraction (XRD) with Cu Kα radiation was performed to characterize framework structures (XRD, Panalytical X’pert PRO MRD, Holland). Thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA) data were collected on a Netzsch STA449F3 system from 30 to 800 °C under an N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. Raman spectra detection was performed using an Ar-ion laser with a wavelength of 532 nm (LabRAM HR800, Horiba JobinYvon). The chemical composition of NiHCF (Na, Ni, and Fe) was determined by inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES, ELAN DRC-e, PerkinElmer). The morphology of the as-prepared samples was recorded via scanning electron microscopy (SEM) (JSM 7600F, JEOL, Japan) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) (JEM-2010F microscope, JEOL, Japan) detection.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Working electrodes were composed of 5 wt % acetylene black, 15 wt % Ketjen black, 70 wt % active material, and 10 wt % poly(tetrafluoroethylene), which were made into a thin film and rolled onto aluminum meshes, with the areal mass loading of active materials of about 5.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Electrochemical tests were carried out in standard CR2032 coin cells. In the half-cells, the counter electrodes were Na metal. In full cells, the hard carbon anode consisted of 10 wt % acetylene black, 80 wt % active material, and 10 wt % poly(vinylidene) difluoride coated onto aluminum foils. To offer a stable charge/discharge platform for the full cells, the mass ratio of anode/cathode was determined to be 1:1. The electrolyte was composed of ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate solution (1:1, vol), 2 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate, and 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub>. All coin cells were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox. The galvanostatic charge/discharge cycle/rate performance was carried on battery test systems (LAND cycler, Wuhan Kingnuo Electronic Co., China). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were performed from 2.0 to 4.2 V at a scanning rate of 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, and 0.7 mV s<sup>–1</sup> on a Princeton electrochemical workstation.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">All of the NiHCF samples were synthesized by the coprecipitation method. Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was used as a chelating agent for controlling the synthesis. According to the change of complexation stability constant by different ratios of C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup>/Ni, the extent of this control changes. The XRD patterns of the as-prepared samples are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a, with remarkable peak splittings situated at 24.5 and 50.4° corresponding to the monoclinic phase. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> With the increasing amount of C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup>, the half-width of these peaks became narrower, indicating a higher crystal periodicity due to a better synthesis control. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Also, the weight loss of the first stage at around 200 °C decreased (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b), indicating tiny amounts of H<sub>2</sub>O molecules in the NiHCFs frameworks. With the temperature increased to 500 °C, the NiHCF frameworks were about to collapse. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The decomposition temperature is higher than that reported for MnHCFs and CoHCFs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> revealing that the as-prepared NiHCFs have a stronger bonded framework structure with better thermal stability. Combining the TGA and ICP results, the composition of NiHCFs is given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, seen as Na<sub>1.16</sub>Ni[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.82</sub>·1.68H<sub>2</sub>O (NiHCF-0), Na<sub>1.27</sub>Ni[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.81</sub>·2.16H<sub>2</sub>O (NiHCF-1), Na<sub>1.66</sub>Ni[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.84</sub>·2.62H<sub>2</sub>O (NiHCF-2), and Na<sub>2.01</sub>Ni[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.85</sub>·1.61H<sub>2</sub>O (NiHCF-3). Without C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> serving as a controlling agent, the coprecipitation reaction process occurs simultaneously and rapidly; thus, NiHCF-0 is present with high contents of vacancies and crystal water molecules. With the participation of C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup>, the competitive reaction between C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> and Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4–</sup> slows down the coprecipitation process, producing highly crystallized, high Na content, and low-vacancy NiHCFs. The Rietveld refinements (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), according to all of the XRD profiles, index to a monoclinic lattice with <i>P</i>21/<i>n</i> symmetry. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The monoclinic frames are different from the traditional face-centered cubic phase by the accommodation of more Na ions in frameworks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c), accompanied by an enlarged skeleton of NiHCF frameworks. In this work, all the samples present a high Na content, with more than 1.1 Na per formula (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), which causes the skeleton variation and results in a monoclinic structure, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> and is caused by the high concentration of Na ions in the precursor solutions. With 2.01-Na per formula, NiHCF-3 is indexed as a monoclinic structure, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, with the lattice constant of <i>a</i> = 10.3025 Å, <i>b</i> = 7.3835 Å, <i>c</i> = 7.3226(4) Å, and β = 91.662°.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/medium/am9b10312_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns and (b) TGA curves of as-prepared samples, NiHCF-0 (black), NiHCF-1 (green), NiHCF-2 (blue), and NiHCF-3 (red). (c) PBA framework distortion from the cubic to monoclinic structure and (d) the local structure of monoclinic-phased NiHCF-3 stemmed from Rietveld refinements.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b10312&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology of the as-prepared NiHCF-3 by SEM and TEM investigation is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. The NiHCF-3 sample is regular cubic-shaped, border-rich particles with a clear boundary and smooth surface. The energy-dispersive spectrometry (EDS) mappings of Na, Ni, Fe, C, and N in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c show a uniform element distribution. According to the ratio of Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to NiCl<sub>2</sub> (C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup>/Ni = 1, 2, 3), the stability constants of coordination compounds (lg β<sub><i>n</i></sub>) are determined to be 5.16, 7.74, and 8.5 at ambient temperatures and pressures. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Generally, the greater the stability constant, the stronger is the binding of the ligand to the metal ion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> By mixing NiCl<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, Ni<sup>2+</sup> ions are preferentially coordinated with C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> ions. Then, Ni<sup>2+</sup> ions were released slowly from the complex and the free Ni<sup>2+</sup> ions coprecipitated with hexacyanoferrate groups to form initial nuclei of NiHCFs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref14 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref14 ref16" aria-label="reference 12, 14 and 16">(12,14,16)</a> The stability constant reflects the affinity of chelating agents to metal ions in chelates. With increasing amounts of C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup>, the chemical species exhibits more efficiency in controlling the synthesis. NiHCFs produced with or without smaller amounts of C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> manifested as extremely irregular particles that agglomerated together without clear demarcations (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). Overall, C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> is a kind of effective chelating agent for controlling the synthesis of highly crystallized, low-vacancy, and well-dispersed NiHCF particles.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/medium/am9b10312_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) SEM, (b) TEM images, and (c) EDS mappings of NiHCF-3 nanoparticles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b10312&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The kinetics of the synthesis process was examined by in situ infrared spectroscopy using C≡N as a probe group, which only interacts with the coordinate metals at both ends. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> The FTIR spectra versus time images of the reaction process both without and with C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a,b, respectively, with the color gradient indicating the intensity of the peak. Looking at the synthesis with C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, it is apparent that the intensity of the new peak is significantly lower than that in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a at the same time, indicating a lower rate of coprecipitation reactions. As seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>, the blank Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> solution shows a single sharp absorption peak at a wavelength 2034 cm<sup>–1</sup> belonging to the stretching mode of C≡N coordinating with Fe<sup>2+</sup> in free Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4–</sup> groups. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> After the addition of Ni<sup>2+</sup> ions, an excessive decrease of the ν(CN) peaks at 2034 cm<sup>–1</sup> is observed, and a new, strong peak emerges at 2088 cm<sup>–1</sup> for the ν(CN) mode of Fe–C≡N–Ni emerges. When Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4–</sup> groups coordinate with Ni<sup>2+</sup> ions, covalent bonds are formed, N and Ni atoms share electrons, and the inner molecular delocalization of N toward Ni takes place, which enhances the C≡N bond strength and shifts the ν(CN) vibrations to higher values. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref20" aria-label="reference 17 and 20">(17,20)</a> The whole reaction process is reflected by the intensity evolution of the stretching frequency of Fe–C≡N–Ni at 2088 cm<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). In the first 20 s, both precipitation solutions go through a burst nucleation process, with C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> exhibiting slight effects at this stage. Without C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> control the constantly enhanced relative intensity indicates that there is a new generation of Fe–C≡N–Ni skeletons emerging with prolonged time. The nucleation and crystal growth processes are intertwined, which causes irregular-shaped aggregation particles without clear boundaries. With C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> control, the relative intensity remained nearly unchanged after the nucleation process, which reveals that nuclei aggregate and merge without the generation of more Fe–C≡N–Ni skeletons throughout the aging process and yields a regularly shaped, highly crystallized, and border-rich NiHCF product, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e. As reported, the border-rich morphology is conducive to form a passivation layer and leads to an enhanced PBA structure stability during the charge/discharge process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> As an intermediate substance, Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> will not affect the final state of the infrared spectrum curve (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> but it does slows down the coordination rate of Ni<sup>2+</sup> ions and hexacyanoferrate groups. The in situ FTIR detection provides direct evidence that oxalate is an effective agent for controlling the synthesis of highly crystallized NiHCF and it mainly affects the crystal growth process. According to previous reports, the critical stability constants between transition metals and chelating agents are almost in the range of 4–9 <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref14 ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref14 ref16" aria-label="reference 12, 14 and 16">(12,14,16)</a> and some potentially available ligand can be provided, indicating that the control mechanism can be verified by in situ FTIR tests.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/medium/am9b10312_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. In situ infrared spectroscopy during the synthesis process (a) without complexing agent control and (b) with C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> assistance. (c) FTIR spectra of the preparation solution at the initial/final state, (d) intensity evolution of FTIR bands at 2088 cm<sup>–1</sup> of Ni–N≡C–Fe. (e) Illustration of the coprecipitation process with/without C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b10312&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performances of the as-prepared NiHCFs are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of four samples were measured at a current density of 0.2C (17 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). The specific capacities of controlled synthesis NiHCFs (73.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for NiHCF-2 and 68.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> for NiHCF-1) are higher than those of NiHCF-0 (55.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). The reduced capacity is due to the loss of activity sites by the emergence of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies. In particular, NiHCF-3 exhibits the highest specific capacity of 86.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which is close to its theoretical value. Owing to the open-framework structure, NiHCF, with large migration channels, provides rapid Na<sup>+</sup> ion transition kinetics for a high rate performance. As seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, NiHCF-3 exhibits a great capacity retention performance of 86.3, 81.4, 79.3, 77.8, 76.1, and 74.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2C, 0.5C, 1C, 2C, 5C, and 10C, respectively, and then recovers to 74.8, 76.3, 78.1, 79.3, 80.8, and 85.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> as the current density shifts back, demonstrating a superior reversibility capacity. While NiHCFs with increasing amounts of water molecules and Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies are present in the frameworks, lattice periodicities are destroyed and migration channels are blocked, leading to poor rate performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Therefore, it is of great importance to obtain a highly crystallized NiHCF with low vacancies. The CV curves of NiHCF-3 at scanning rates from 0.1 to 0.7 mV s<sup>–1</sup> within 2.0 to 4.0 V demonstrate a low polarization. The oxidation/redox peaks at 3.38/3.17 V correspond to the redox reaction of Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup>, suggesting superior reversibility and outstanding rate performance. These are also verified by the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of NiHCF at high rates seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. It is noticeable that NiHCF-3 shows remarkable capacity retention of 64.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (74.6%) even at 4250 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (50C); the corresponding galvanostatic charge/discharge curves are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. The long-term cycle performances are performed at a current density of 170 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The four NiHCF samples exhibit superior capacity stabilities, with capacity retentions of 90.4, 86.2, 84,8, and 78.0% over 800 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>) for NiHCF-3, NiHCF-2, NiHCF-1, and NiHCF-0, respectively. The one-electron transfer reactions hardly bring any variation in the frameworks and keep the stability of the NiHCF materials in redox reactions, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> which leads to these stable reversible reactions of the four NiHCF samples. While the increasing Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies and water molecules cause the fragile skeletons and side reactions during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction process, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> NiHCF-0, NiHCF-1, and NiHCF-2 exhibit inferior capacity stability compared with NiHCF-3. The excellent electrochemical performance of NiHCF-3 further confirms the high quality of PBA cathodes with fewer vacancies, fewer water molecules, and higher crystallinity.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/medium/am9b10312_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performances of as-prepared samples. (a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge curves at a current density of 17 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, (b) rate performance, and (e) cycling performance at 2C (current density = 170 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). NiHCF-0 (black), NiHCF-1 (green), NiHCF-2 (blue), and NiHCF-3 (red), respectively. (c) CV curves and (d) galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of NiHCF-3 at different rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b10312&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">For practical applications, the NiHCF/hard carbon full cells were fabricated, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. Before the full cells assembly, a Na insertion process was necessary for the preparation of the hard carbon anode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The charge/discharge curve for the NiHCF-3 shows the plateau of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> at 3.3 V; the hard carbon/Na half-cell shows a voltage platform when the voltage is lower than 0.1 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). The specific capacity of HC is about 325 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which is significantly higher than the cathode material (∼85 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). To offer a stable charge/discharge platform for the full cells, the hard carbon anode was set at a certain capacity excess to the NiHCF cathode and only the platform part of the hard carbon served in redox reactions. The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of the full cells at different current densities are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. The full cell shows superior electrochemical behavior with a discharge capacity of 78.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2C (based on the mass of the active material) and a maintaining capacity of 73.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 2C corresponds to the capacity retention as high as 93.7% with a high Na<sup>+</sup> insertion platform at ∼3.1 V and a great rate performance. The excellent long-term cycle performance of the full cell is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, with an extremely high capacity retention of 95.2% over 1000 cycles and the Coulombic efficiency approaching 100% in the whole cyclic process. With the high reaction voltage, the specific energy is calculated to be 210.5 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> for such fabricated NiHCF//HC full cells as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. Although there is no obvious capacity attenuation, the specific energy is decreased. As seen in the inset charge/discharge curves in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7c</a>, the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion reaction specific capacity remained at 69.7 in the 1000th cycle from 73.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the initial time. The reaction voltage decreased from 3.2 to 2.8 V. With the ultrastability of NiHCF cathodes, the voltage decrease is ascribed to the enhanced potential (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) of the hard carbon anode by the consumption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in cyclic reactions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The as-assembled full cells exhibit specific energy retention of 166.9 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> (79.3%) at the 1000th cycle. Compared with previous reports (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 16 and 38 to 44">(16,38−44)</a> NiHCF-HC full cells in this work present a competitive specific energy and noticeable, long-cyclic performance, which is essential for practical application.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/medium/am9b10312_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Full cell (NiHCF-3/HC) electrochemical performances with the mass ratio of anode/cathode equal to 1. (a) Voltage profiles of the NiHCF-3 cathode and hard carbon anode, (b) galvanostatic charge/discharge curves at different rates, and (c) long-term cycle performance at a current density of 170 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (calculated from the cathode active mass).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b10312&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/medium/am9b10312_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Specific energy and cyclic performance in this work compared to previously reported PBA cathode full cells (calculated by the cathode active mass). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref28 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref28 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 16, 28 and 38 to 44">(16,28,38−44)</a></p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/asset/images/large/am9b10312_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b10312&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The ultrastable NiHCF-3 cathode with a low content of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4–</sup> vacancies and water molecules and a high content of Na<sup>+</sup> ions are synthesized by a facile oxalate-controlled method. Via in situ infrared spectroscopy detection, it was verified that the addition of the C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>–2</sup> chelating agent can effectively control the synthetic kinetics. The competitive reaction between C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>2–</sup> and Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4–</sup> slightly slows down the nucleation process and mainly inhibits the new generation of Fe–C≡N–Ni skeletons during the aging process. Benefitting from high crystallinity and few [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] defects, NiHCF-3 exhibits a good reversible capacity of 86.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, excellent rate capability with a remarkable capacity retention of 64.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (74.6%) at 50C, and a superior long-term cycling ability of 90% capacity retention over 800 cycles at a current density of 170 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, the full cells were fabricated with the NiHCF-3 cathode and hard carbon anode which delivered a capacity of 75 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> without noticeable capacity decay, even over 1000 cycles. The in situ infrared spectroscopy-assisted synthetization can be developed to explore effective chelating ligands for high-performance PBA cathode materials applied in large-scale energy storage systems.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b10312" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b10312</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Rietveld refinement results; XRD patterns; SEM images; FT-IR spectra; charge/discharge curves; lattice parameters and chemical compositions; additional electrochemical data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b10312/suppl_file/am9b10312_si_001.pdf">am9b10312_si_001.pdf (912.56 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b10312" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701428"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, J.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Prussian Blue Analogs as Battery Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hurlbutt, Kevin; Wheeler, Samuel; Capone, Isaac; Pasta, Mauro</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Joule</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1950-1960</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JOULBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2542-4351</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Cell Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian blue analogs have significant promise as active materials for the next generation of battery electrodes with improved cycle life and rate capability.  Their useful electrochem. properties include two independent redox centers per unit cell; a nanoporous, open framework for rapid ion conduction; high stability during ion (de)insertion; and structural and electrochem. tunability for diverse applications.  Here we share insights into how control of the five main crystallog. features (two transition-metal ions, the inserting ion, defects, and water) imparts control over the ion-insertion reaction.  We then identify five key opportunities to expand our understanding of these materials, including the role of water in their ion conduction, modeling, synthesis methods, use as anode materials, and technoeconomics.  Further research in these areas will accelerate the development of new, high-performing battery electrodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501727"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y. J.; Schwarzenbach, D.; Petter, W.; Ludi, A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Inorganic Chemistry</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
