    </div><div class="casAbstract">Single crystals of Prussian Blue, were grown by very slow diffusion of water vapor into a soln. of Fe3+ and Fe(CN)64- in concd. HCl.  The structure of this compd. was detd. by x-ray diffraction.  The crystals are cubic, with a 10.166 Å; d.(exptl.) = 1.75-1.81 and d.(calcd.) = 1.78 for Z = 1 and x = 15.  The Fe(CN)6 positions are only partly occupied.  Partial ordering of the corresponding vacancies, possibly related to the conditions for crystal growths, causes deviation from Fm3m symmetry.  The least-squares refinement in the space group Pm3m for three different crystals gave R factors of 0.032, 0.042, and 0.046, resp.  The corresponding av. distances are Fe(II)-C 1.92, C-N 1.13, and Fe(III)-N 2.03 Å.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1021/ic50206a032"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Herren, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fischer, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ludi, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Halg, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Neutron diffraction study of Prussian Blue, Fe4[Fe(CN)6]3·xH2O.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian Blue, Fe4[Fe(CN)6]3·xH2O (x = 14-16), was studied by powder neutron diffraction in four different states of hydration: dehydrated, x(H,D)2O with a vanishing scattering contribution of H, xD2O, and xH2O.  Structural calcns. using diffraction profile anal. reveal two structurally distinguishable kinds of water mols.  Six mols. of water are coordinated to Fe(III) at empty N sites; approx. eight addnl. water mols. are present either as isolated mols. at the center of the unit cell octants or as water mols. connected by H bonds to the coordinated ones.  The corresponding O-D-O distance is 2.87 Å.  The disordered overall structure is described as a superposition of various ordered substructures.  Magnetic contributions to the neutron intensities below the Curie temp. of 5.6 K reveal ferromagnetism.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly Crystallized Na2CoFe(CN)6 with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Xianyong; Wu, Chenghao; Wei, Congxiao; Hu, Ling; Qian, Jiangfeng; Cao, Yuliang; Ai, Xinping; Wang, Jiulin; Yang, Hanxi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian blue and its analogs have received particular attention as superior cathodes for Na-ion batteries due to their potential 2-Na storage capacity (∼170 mA-h g-1) and low cost.  However, most of the Prussian blue compds. obtained from the conventional synthetic routes contain large amts. of Fe(CN)6 vacancies and coordinated H2O mols., which leads to the collapse of cyano-bridged framework and serious deterioration of their Na-storage ability.  Herein, the authors propose a facile citrate-assisted controlled crystn. method to obtain low-defect Prussian blue lattice with greatly improved Na-storage capacity and cycling stability.  As an example, the as-prepd. Na2CoFe(CN)6 nanocrystals demonstrate a reversible 2-Na storage reaction with a high specific capacity of 150 mA-h g-1 and a ∼ 90% capacity retention over 200 cycles, possibly serving as a low cost and high performance cathode for Na-ion batteries.  In particular, the synthetic strategy described in this work may be extended to other coordination-framework materials for a wide range of energy conversion and storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2015.02.006"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Low-defect Prussian blue nanocubes as high capacity and long life cathodes for aqueous Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Xianyong; Luo, Yang; Sun, Mengying; Qian, Jiangfeng; Cao, Yuliang; Ai, Xinping; Yang, Hanxi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian blue compds. have potential advantages of high working potentials and multi-electron redox capacities as Na-ion battery cathodes; however, they suffer from their poor capacity utilization in aq. electrolytes due to the blockage of the active lattice sites by coordinating and zeolitic water for Na insertion reaction.  To overcome this problem, we synthesized low-defect Prussian blue FeFe(CN)6 nanocrystals and revealed their Na-storage performance in aq. electrolytes.  Benefitting from its suppressed lattice defects and well-defined nanocubic morphol., the as-prepd. Prussian blue nanocrystals exhibit a greatly improved capacity of ∼125 mAh g-1, a strong rate capability with 102 mAh g-1 at a high rate of 20C and a remarkably long cycle life with 83% capacity retention over 500 cycles, exceeding considerably the aq. Na-storage cathodes reported so far and capably serving as a high performance cathode for aq. Na-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The nucleation rate plays a crit. role in the synthesis of Prussian blue analogs.  Rapid pptn. may lead to a large no. of vacancies and a large amt. of interstitial H2O in the material, resulting in poor electrochem. performance in batteries.  Hence, Na citrate is used to compete with [Fe(CN)6]4- to slow down the coordination rates of Ni2+ and Mn2+ ions with ferrous cyanide ions. Most noteworthy, the Coulombic efficiency and specific capacity gradually increase in the 1st few cycles, which can be ascribed to the formation of a passivation layer on the surface of the electrode.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors present a joint exptl. and computational study of the hexacyanoferrate aq. complexes at equil. in the 250 meV to 7.15 keV regime.  The expts. and the computations include the vibrational spectroscopy of the cyanide ligands, the valence electronic absorption spectra and Fe 1s core hole spectra using element-specific resonant x-ray absorption and emission techniques.  D. functional theory (DFT) based quantum mechanics/mol. mechanics (QM/MM) mol. dynamics (MD) simulations are performed to generate explicit solute-solvent configurations, which serve as inputs for the spectroscopy calcns. of the expts. spanning the IR to x-ray wavelengths.  The spectroscopy simulations are performed at the same level of theory across this large energy window, which allows for a systematic comparison of the effects of explicit solute-solvent interactions in the vibrational, valence electronic and core-level spectra of hexacyanoferrate complexes in H2O.  While the spectroscopy of hexacyanoferrate complexes in soln. were the subject of several studies, most of the previous works have focused on a narrow energy window and have not accounted for explicit solute-solvent interactions in their spectroscopy simulations.  The authors focus the anal. on identifying how the local solvation environment around the hexacyanoferrate complexes influences the intensity and line shape of specific spectroscopic features in the UV/visible, x-ray absorption and valence-to-core X-ray emission spectra.  The identification of these features and their relation to solute-solvent interactions is important because hexacyanoferrate complexes serve as model systems for understanding the photochem. and photophysics of a large class of Fe(II) and Fe(III) complexes in soln.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1038/s41929-019-0226-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Miao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuld, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sehested, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aoki, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Camellone, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Cu/CeO2 catalysts are highly active for the low-temp. water-gas shift-a core reaction in syngas chem. for tuning the H2/CO/CO2 proportions in feed streams-but the direct identification and quant. description of the active sites remain challenging.  Here we report that the active copper clusters consist of a bottom layer of mainly Cu+ atoms bonded on the oxygen vacancies (Ov) of ceria, in a form of Cu+-Ov-Ce 3+ , and a top layer of Cu0 atoms coordinated with the underlying Cu+ atoms.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This study evaluates the effect of equimolar substitution of manganese by cobalt or nickel in hexacyanoferrate (HCF) open frameworks as electrode materials for Na-ion batteries.  As the stability of Mn-N bonds is crucial to obtain long term stability and cyclability of manganese (Mn-HCF), the samples were analyzed thoroughly using several spectroscopic and structural methods.  The XPS and IR expts. reveal that the charge d. around Fe is modulated by the presence of Co or Ni, which is assocd. with their high polarizing power, leading to decreased cell distortion as revealed by XRD.  The Rietveld refinement demonstrated that the octahedra built by 3d metals and the cyanide nitrogen were distorted with the axial bond distances being larger than the equatorial distances.  This octahedral distortion promotes the spin behavior of 3/2 for Mn2+ confirmed by magnetic expts.; the arising of this spin state is attributed to d orbital splitting detd. by UV-Vis expts.  Therefore, the changes upon Mn substitution are related to the modification of the covalent character of Mn-N bonds, modulated by the effect of the Ni and Co polarizing power.  All these properties improve the electrochem. stability of the Ni or Co substituted materials as Na-ion batteries, leading to higher capacity retention even at higher C-rates (5C) and good capacity recovery, in comparison with those obtained for Mn-HCF.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1016/j.matchemphys.2017.09.029"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gerber, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A series 4 metal hexacyanoferrates, of KxMy[Fe(CN)6]z·qH2O, with x, y, z and q representing stoichiometric nos. and M = Fe (1), Co (2), Ni (3) and Cu (4), were prepd. by a simple co-pptn. reaction.  The yields of 1-4 are a function of the Pauling scale electronegativity, σM, the order of increasing yield of the metal hexacyanoferrates are 1 (M = Fe, 26% yield, σM = 1.83) &lt; 2 (M = Co, 43% yield, σM = 1.88) &lt; 4 (M = Cu, 85% yield, σM = 1.90) &lt; 3 (M = Ni, 94% yield, σM = 1.91). Each stretching frequency at the different wavenumbers represents a different possible combination of the different oxidn. states e.g.  FeII-C≃N-FeII, FeIII-C≃N-FeII, FeIII-C≃N-FeIII and FeII-C≃N-FeIII.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-Performance Hard Carbon Anode: Tunable Local Structures and Sodium Storage Mechanism</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Yu; Sun, Shixiong; Ou, Mingyang; Liu, Yi; Fan, Chenyang; Sun, Xueping; Peng, Jian; Li, Yuyu; Qiu, Yuegang; Wei, Peng; Deng, Zhi; Xu, Yue; Han, Jiantao; Huang, Yunhui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Energy Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon (HC) is one of the most promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its suitable potential and high reversible capacity.  At the same time, the correlation between carbon local structure and sodium-ion storage behavior is not clearly understood.  In this paper, the two series of HC materials with perfect spherical morphol. and tailored microstructures were designed and successfully produced using resorcinol formaldehyde (RF) resin as precursor.  Via hydrothermal self-assembly and controlled pyrolysis, RF is a flexible precursor for high-purity carbon with a wide range of local-structure variation.  Using these processes, one series of five representative RF-based HC nanospheres with varying degrees of graphitization were obtained from an RF precursor at different carbonization temps.  The other series of HC materials with various microscopic carbon layer lengths and shapes was achieved by carbonizing five RF precursors with different crosslinking degrees at a single carbonization condition (1300 °C and 2 h).  On the basis of the microstructures, unique electrochem. characteristics, and at. pair distribution function (PDF) analyses, we proposed a new model of "three-phase" structural for HC materials and found triregion Na-ion storage behavior: chemi-/physisorption, intercalation between carbon layers, and pore-filling, derived from the HC phases, resp.  These results enable new understanding and insight into the sodium storage mechanism in HC materials and improve the potential for carbon-based SIB anodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref23" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b01132"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The Prussian Blue analog, NaxFeMn(CN)6, is a potential new cathode material for Na-ion batteries.  During Na intercalation, the dehydrated material exhibits a monoclinic to rhombohedral phase transition, while the hydrated material remains in the monoclinic phase.  With d. functional theory calcns., the phase transition is explained in terms of a competition between Coulomb attraction, Pauli repulsion, and d-π covalent bonding.  The interstitial Na cations have a strong Coulomb attraction to the N anions in the host material, which tend to bend the Mn-N bonds and reduce the vol. of the structure.  The presence of lattice H2O enhances the Pauli repulsion so that the vol. redn. is suppressed.  The calcd. vol. change, as it depends upon the presence of lattice H2O, is consistent with exptl. measurements.  Addnl., a new LiFeMn(CN)6 phase is predicted where MnN6 octahedra decomp. into LiN4 and MnN4 edge-sharing tetrahedra.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1149/2.0701706jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rudola, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Balaya, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Monoclinic Sodium Iron Hexacyanoferrate Cathode and Non-Flammable Glyme-Based Electrolyte for Inexpensive Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Sodium-ion batteries using earth-abundant Fe or Ti based cathodes and anodes would be ideal candidates for such storage systems.  Herein, a new phase of Na-rich and all Fe Prussian Blue Analog, monoclinic Na2Fe2(CN)6.2H2O, is reported as a potential cathode for such grid-storage sodium-ion batteries.  This water-insol. and air-stable cathode can deliver 85 mAh g-1 at an av. discharge voltage of 3 V vs. Na/Na+ with excellent cycle life (3,000 cycles). Its compatibility with a newly developed non-flammable glyme-based liq. electrolyte, 1M NaBF4 in tetraglyme, is also disclosed along with general electrochem. and thermal characterization of this electrolyte for sodium-ion battery application.  Finally, three different types of full cells are revealed with either monoclinic or rhombohedral phase as cathode and graphite or the recently reported Na2Ti3O7 ↹ Na3-xTi3O7 pathway of Na2Ti3O7 as anode.  Full cell energy densities of 70-90 Wh kg-1 (using cumulative cathode and anode wts.) could be obtained without any pre-cycling steps. Andrew; Yang, Wanli; Toney, Michael F.; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian Blue analogs (PBAs) have shown promise as electrode materials for grid-scale batteries because of their high cycle life and rapid kinetics in aq.-based electrolytes.  However, these materials suffer from relatively low specific capacity, which may limit their practical applications.  Here, we investigate strategies to improve the specific capacity of these materials while maintaining their cycling stability and elucidate mechanisms that enhance their electrochem. properties.  In particular, we have studied the electrochem. and structural properties of manganese hexacyanoferrate (MnHCFe) and cobalt hexacyanoferrate (CoHCFe) in an aq., sodium-ion electrolyte.  We also studied manganese-cobalt hexacyanoferrate (Mn-CoHCFe) solid solns. with different Mn/Co ratios that combine properties of both MnHCFe and CoHCFe.  The materials have the characteristic open-framework crystal structure of PBAs, and their specific capacities can be significantly improved by electrochem. cycling (oxidizing and reducing) both the carbon-coordinated Fe and the nitrogen-coordinated Co or Mn ions.  In situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction studies and ex situ soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy combined with an in-depth electrochem. characterization provide insight into the different electrochem. properties assocd. with the Fe, Co, and Mn redox couples.  We show that cycling the C-coordinated Fe preserves the crystal structure and enables the outstanding kinetics and cycle life previously displayed by PBAs in aq. electrolytes.  On the other hand, the N-coordinated Co and Mn ions exhibit a slower kinetic regime due to structural distortions resulting from the weak N-coordinated crystal field, but they still contribute significantly towards increasing the specific capacity of the materials.  These results provide the understanding needed to drive future development of PBAs for grid-scale applications that require extremely high cycle life and kinetics.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1021/ja512383b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Henkelman, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium is globally available, which makes a sodium-ion rechargeable battery preferable to a lithium-ion battery for large-scale storage of elec. energy, provided a host cathode for Na can be found that provides the necessary capacity, voltage, and cycle life at the prescribed charge/discharge rate.  Low-cost hexacyanometallates are promising cathodes because of their ease of synthesis and rigid open framework that enables fast Na+ insertion and extn.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium iron hexacyanoferrate with high Na content as a Na-rich cathode material for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">You, Ya; Yu, Xiqian; Yin, Yaxia; Nam, Kyung-Wan; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Research</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the worldwide abundance and low-cost of Na, room-temp. Na-ion batteries are emerging as attractive energy storage systems for large-scale grids.  Increasing the Na content in cathode materials is one of the effective ways to achieve high energy d.  Prussian blue and its analogs (PBAs) are promising Na-rich cathode materials since they can theor. store two Na+ ions per formula unit.  However, increasing the Na content in PBAs cathode materials remains a major challenge.  Here we show that sodium iron hexacyanoferrate with high Na content can be obtained by simply controlling the reducing agent and reaction atm. during synthesis. This Na-rich sodium iron hexacyanoferrate demonstrates a high specific capacity of 150 mAh·g-1 and remarkable cycling performance with 90% capacity retention after 200 cycles.  Furthermore, the Na intercalation/de-intercalation mechanism has been systematically studied by in situ Raman spectroscopy, X-ray diffraction and X-ray absorption spectroscopy anal. for the first time.  The Na-rich sodium iron hexacyanoferrate can function as a plenteous Na reservoir and has great potential as a cathode material for practical Na-ion batteries.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2001</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Society of Agronomy</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The presence of toxic metals in natural environments presents a potential health hazard for humans.  Metal contaminants in these environments are usually tightly bound to colloidal particles and org. matter.  This represents a major constraint to their removal using currently available in situ remediation technologies.  One technique that has shown potential for facilitated metal removal from soil is treatment with an anionic microbial surfactant, rhamnolipid.  Successful application of rhamnolipid in metal removal requires knowledge of the rhamnolipid-metal complexation reaction.  Therefore, our objective was to evaluate the biosurfactant complexation affinity for the most common natural soil and water cations and for various metal contaminants.  The conditional stability const. (log K) for each of these metals was detd. using an ion-exchange resin technique.  Results show the measured stability consts. follow the order (from strongest to weakest): Al3+ &gt;Cu2+ &gt;Pb2+ &gt;Cd2+ &gt;Zn2+ &gt;Fe3+ &gt;Hg2+ &gt;Ca2+ &gt;Co2+ &gt;Ni2+ &gt;Mn2+ &gt;Mg2+ &gt;K+.  These data indicate that rhamnolipid will preferentially complex metal contaminants such as Pb, Cd, and Hg in the presence of common soil or water cations.  The measured rhamnolipid-metal stability consts. were found in most cases to be similar or higher than conditional stability consts. reported in the literature for metal complexation with acetic acid, oxalic acid, citric acid, and fulvic acids.  These results help delineate the conditions under which rhamnolipid may be successfully applied as a remediation agent in the removal of metal contaminants from soil, as well as surface waters, groundwater, and waste streams.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref32" class="refNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit32" data-doi="10.1021/ac961121d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pharr, C. The IR spectra of electrochem. modulated species were measured by a step-scan FTIR spectrometer.  The phase-sensitive detection capability of this technique was used to provide information about dynamic electrode processes and to enhance sensitivity to surface species.  The cause of decreases in the heterogeneous rate const., k°, of the ferricyanide/ferrocyanide redox process with time was studied.  Previous authors offer a variety of opinions for the variation of k° for this reaction including the adsorption of polymeric hexacyanoferrate complexes, ferrocyanide, or ferricyanide on the electrode surface; other reports indicate that no species adsorb on the electrode during the electron-transfer process. In addn., the as-prepd. sample with rhombohedral phase demonstrated lower band gap and lower energy barrier for Na+ ions insertion.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Low cost and high performance Li-ion batteries have been extensively pursued for grid-scale energy storage applications, however, their development has been impeded for a long time due to the lack of qualified cathode materials with not only decent electrochem. performance but also resource abundance and low price.  In this paper, we report Prussian-blue type FeFe(CN)6 nanocrystals with well-controlled lattice defects and perfect nanocubic morphol., which can exhibit a high Li-storage capacity of 160 mAh/g, a strong rate performance at 24 C, and a superior cycle stability with 90% capacity retention over 300 cycles.  This low defect lattice and its excellent Li-insertion performance might provide a new insight into the design of advanced Li-ion battery materials and also a competitive alternative to the presently developed Li+ insertion cathodes to develop low cost and high performance Li-ion batteries for grid-scale energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref35" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b11070"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">On the Mechanism of the Improved Operation Voltage of Rhombohedral Nickel Hexacyanoferrate as Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">On the Mechanism of the Improved Operation Voltage of Rhombohedral Nickel Hexacyanoferrate as Cathodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ji, Zhuan; Han, Bo; Liang, Haitao; Zhou, Chenggang; Gao, Qiang; Xia, Kaisheng; Wu, Jinping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors reported a rhombohedral Na-rich nickel hexacyanoferrate (r-NiHCF) with high discharge voltage, which also possesses long cycle stability and excellent rate capability when serving as the cathode material of Na-ion batteries.  First-principles calcns. suggest that the high working voltage of r-NiHCF is correlated to the asym. residence of Na+ ions in the rhombohedral framework in parallel with the low charge d. at the Fe2+ ions.  In both aq. and ether-based electrolytes, r-NiHCF exhibits higher voltage than that of cubic NiHCF.  Rate and cycle expts. indicate that r-NiHCF delivers a specific capacity of 66.8 mAh g-1 at the c.d. of 80 mA g-1, which is approx. to the theor. capacity of r-NiHCF.  A capacity retention of 96% can be achieved after 200 cycles.  The excellent stability of r-NiHCF can be assigned to the absence of rhombohedral-cubic phase transition and negligible vol. variation during electrochem. redox, as proven by the ex situ XRD patterns at different depths of charge/ discharge and the DFT calcns., resp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref36" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1149/2.0031610jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, E.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian blue compds. (PBs) have been intensely studied as promising cathode materials for Na ion batteries due to their rigid open framework which benefits Na+ insertion/extn.  However, the existence of Fe(CN)6 vacancies occupied by coordinating H2O in the framework has great influence on the electrochem. performance.  The authors prepd. PB compds. using simple soln. pptn. method at 0°, 40°C and 80°C, resp.  The obtained Na1.73Fe[Fe(CN)6]0.98.box.0.02 (PB0), Na1.27Fe[Fe(CN)6]0.94.box.0.06 (PB40) and Na1.12Fe[Fe(CN)6]0.92.box.0.08 (PB80) samples were studied to clarify the effects of Fe(CN)6 vacancies and the coordinating H2O mols. on the electrochem. behavior.  CV tests indicated that coordinating H2O could be gradually extd. and attend side reaction at ∼4.0V, which led to impedance growth and caused capacity decrease during cycling.  Dehydrated PB samples showed increased reversible capacities.  However, the cycling stability of the PBs mainly depended on the perfection of the lattice structure.  Low defect structure Na1.73Fe[Fe(CN)6]0.98.box.0.02 exhibited superior cycling stability with initial reversible capacity of 105 mA h g-1 (100 mA g-1) and intact cubic phase lattice structure during long term cycling.  Dehydrated Na1.73Fe[Fe(CN)6]0.98.box.0.02 showed further improved reversible capacities of 123 mA h g-1 (100 mA g-1) with 73% capacity retention after 200 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref37" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1126/science.aam6014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries have been an active area of research over the past several years. FeOx nanoparticles supported on carbon nanotubes as the anode material for sodium-ion batteries.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A low-cost and environmentally benign aqueous rechargeable sodium-ion battery based on NaTi2(PO4)3-Na2NiFe(CN)6 intercalation chemistry</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Xianyong; Cao, Yuliang; Ai, Xinping; Qian, Jiangfeng; Yang, Hanxi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemistry Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">31</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">145-148</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ECCMF9</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1388-2481</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">An aq. rechargable Na-ion battery is developed by use of Na-deficient NaTi2(PO4)3 anode, Na-rich Na2NiFe(CN)6 cathode and aq. Na2SO4 electrolyte.  This battery system can give an av. output voltage of 1.27 V, a energy d. of 42.5 Wh kg-1 and can retain 88% of initial capacity for 250 cycles cycled at the 5 C rate.  Moreover, this aq. Na-ion battery has the advantages of low cost, environmentally friendliness and inherent safety, particularly attractive for grid-scale energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref41" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1039/C4CC05830E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A structure optimized Prussian blue analog Na1.76Ni0.12Mn0.88[Fe(CN)6]0.98 (PBMN) is synthesized and investigated.                         rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701428"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Cell Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian blue analogs have significant promise as active materials for the next generation of battery electrodes with improved cycle life and rate capability.  Their useful electrochem. properties include two independent redox centers per unit cell; a nanoporous, open framework for rapid ion conduction; high stability during ion (de)insertion; and structural and electrochem. tunability for diverse applications.  Here we share insights into how control of the five main crystallog. features (two transition-metal ions, the inserting ion, defects, and water) imparts control over the ion-insertion reaction.  We then identify five key opportunities to expand our understanding of these materials, including the role of water in their ion conduction, modeling, synthesis methods, use as anode materials, and technoeconomics.  Further research in these areas will accelerate the development of new, high-performing battery electrodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501727"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Y. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schwarzenbach, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Petter, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ludi, A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The crystal structure of Prussian Blue: Fe4[Fe(CN)6]3·xH2O</span>. <i>Inorg. Chem.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1977</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>16</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2704</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ic50177a008</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The crystal structure of Prussian Blue:  Fe4[Fe(CN)6]3.xH2O</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Buser, H. J.; Schwarzenbach, D.; Petter, W.; Ludi, A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Inorganic Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1977</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2704-10</span>CODEN:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0020-1669</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Single crystals of Prussian Blue, were grown by very slow diffusion of water vapor into a soln. of Fe3+ and Fe(CN)64- in concd. HCl.  The structure of this compd. was detd. by x-ray diffraction.  The crystals are cubic, with a 10.166 Å; d.(exptl.) = 1.75-1.81 and d.(calcd.) = 1.78 for Z = 1 and x = 15.  The Fe(CN)6 positions are only partly occupied.  Partial ordering of the corresponding vacancies, possibly related to the conditions for crystal growths, causes deviation from Fm3m symmetry.  The least-squares refinement in the space group Pm3m for three different crystals gave R factors of 0.032, 0.042, and 0.046, resp.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors present a joint exptl. and computational study of the hexacyanoferrate aq. complexes at equil. in the 250 meV to 7.15 keV regime. D. functional theory (DFT) based quantum mechanics/mol. mechanics (QM/MM) mol. dynamics (MD) simulations are performed to generate explicit solute-solvent configurations, which serve as inputs for the spectroscopy calcns. of the expts. spanning the IR to x-ray wavelengths. While the spectroscopy of hexacyanoferrate complexes in soln. were the subject of several studies, most of the previous works have focused on a narrow energy window and have not accounted for explicit solute-solvent interactions in their spectroscopy simulations. In this paper, the two series of HC materials with perfect spherical morphol. and tailored microstructures were designed and successfully produced using resorcinol formaldehyde (RF) resin as precursor.  Via hydrothermal self-assembly and controlled pyrolysis, RF is a flexible precursor for high-purity carbon with a wide range of local-structure variation. The other series of HC materials with various microscopic carbon layer lengths and shapes was achieved by carbonizing five RF precursors with different crosslinking degrees at a single carbonization condition (1300 °C and 2 h). With d. functional theory calcns., the phase transition is explained in terms of a competition between Coulomb attraction, Pauli repulsion, and d-π covalent bonding. Sodium-ion batteries using earth-abundant Fe or Ti based cathodes and anodes would be ideal candidates for such storage systems.  Herein, a new phase of Na-rich and all Fe Prussian Blue Analog, monoclinic Na2Fe2(CN)6.2H2O, is reported as a potential cathode for such grid-storage sodium-ion batteries.  This water-insol. and air-stable cathode can deliver 85 mAh g-1 at an av. discharge voltage of 3 V vs. Na/Na+ with excellent cycle life (3,000 cycles).  Many facets about its sodium storage characteristics are discussed with particular emphasis on the role of interstitial water on the sodium storage performance and its conversion to the dehydrated rhombohedral phase.  Its compatibility with a newly developed non-flammable glyme-based liq. electrolyte, 1M NaBF4 in tetraglyme, is also disclosed along with general electrochem. and thermal characterization of this electrolyte for sodium-ion battery application.  Finally, three different types of full cells are revealed with either monoclinic or rhombohedral phase as cathode and graphite or the recently reported Na2Ti3O7 ↹ Na3-xTi3O7 pathway of Na2Ti3O7 as anode.  Full cell energy densities of 70-90 Wh kg-1 (using cumulative cathode and anode wts.) could be obtained without any pre-cycling steps.  This new cathode and safe electrolyte may hold great promise toward development of inexpensive, non-flammable and highly stable grid-storage sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.7b00179"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nazar, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian Blue analogs (PBAs) have shown promise as electrode materials for grid-scale batteries because of their high cycle life and rapid kinetics in aq.-based electrolytes.  However, these materials suffer from relatively low specific capacity, which may limit their practical applications.  Here, we investigate strategies to improve the specific capacity of these materials while maintaining their cycling stability and elucidate mechanisms that enhance their electrochem. properties.  In particular, we have studied the electrochem. and structural properties of manganese hexacyanoferrate (MnHCFe) and cobalt hexacyanoferrate (CoHCFe) in an aq., sodium-ion electrolyte.  We also studied manganese-cobalt hexacyanoferrate (Mn-CoHCFe) solid solns. with different Mn/Co ratios that combine properties of both MnHCFe and CoHCFe.  The materials have the characteristic open-framework crystal structure of PBAs, and their specific capacities can be significantly improved by electrochem. cycling (oxidizing and reducing) both the carbon-coordinated Fe and the nitrogen-coordinated Co or Mn ions.  In situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction studies and ex situ soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy combined with an in-depth electrochem. characterization provide insight into the different electrochem. properties assocd. with the Fe, Co, and Mn redox couples.  We show that cycling the C-coordinated Fe preserves the crystal structure and enables the outstanding kinetics and cycle life previously displayed by PBAs in aq. electrolytes.  On the other hand, the N-coordinated Co and Mn ions exhibit a slower kinetic regime due to structural distortions resulting from the weak N-coordinated crystal field, but they still contribute significantly towards increasing the specific capacity of the materials.  These results provide the understanding needed to drive future development of PBAs for grid-scale applications that require extremely high cycle life and kinetics.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref28" class="rightTabRefNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit28" data-doi="10.1021/ja512383b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, X.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Henkelman, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The presence of toxic metals in natural environments presents a potential health hazard for humans.  Metal contaminants in these environments are usually tightly bound to colloidal particles and org. matter.  This represents a major constraint to their removal using currently available in situ remediation technologies.  One technique that has shown potential for facilitated metal removal from soil is treatment with an anionic microbial surfactant, rhamnolipid.  Successful application of rhamnolipid in metal removal requires knowledge of the rhamnolipid-metal complexation reaction.  Therefore, our objective was to evaluate the biosurfactant complexation affinity for the most common natural soil and water cations and for various metal contaminants.  The conditional stability const. (log K) for each of these metals was detd. using an ion-exchange resin technique.  Results show the measured stability consts. follow the order (from strongest to weakest): Al3+ &gt;Cu2+ &gt;Pb2+ &gt;Cd2+ &gt;Zn2+ &gt;Fe3+ &gt;Hg2+ &gt;Ca2+ &gt;Co2+ &gt;Ni2+ &gt;Mn2+ &gt;Mg2+ &gt;K+.  These data indicate that rhamnolipid will preferentially complex metal contaminants such as Pb, Cd, and Hg in the presence of common soil or water cations.  The measured rhamnolipid-metal stability consts. were found in most cases to be similar or higher than conditional stability consts. reported in the literature for metal complexation with acetic acid, oxalic acid, citric acid, and fulvic acids.  These results help delineate the conditions under which rhamnolipid may be successfully applied as a remediation agent in the removal of metal contaminants from soil, as well as surface waters, groundwater, and waste streams.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref32" class="rightTabRefNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit32" data-doi="10.1021/ac961121d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pharr, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">FTIR spectrometry adds complementary structural information to sensitive electroanal. measurements.  In situ, external reflection FTIR techniques allowed for examn. of interfacial electrochem. events through a thin-layer spectroelectrochem. cell in which a sinusoidally modulated potential was applied to the electrode.  The IR spectra of electrochem. modulated species were measured by a step-scan FTIR spectrometer.  The phase-sensitive detection capability of this technique was used to provide information about dynamic electrode processes and to enhance sensitivity to surface species.  The cause of decreases in the heterogeneous rate const., k°, of the ferricyanide/ferrocyanide redox process with time was studied.  Previous authors offer a variety of opinions for the variation of k° for this reaction including the adsorption of polymeric hexacyanoferrate complexes, ferrocyanide, or ferricyanide on the electrode surface; other reports indicate that no species adsorb on the electrode during the electron-transfer process.  The authors' results indicate that, with KCl as electrolyte, a hexacyanoferrate complex can form during potential cycling in the ferri-/ferrocyanide redox couple, but only under rather extreme exptl. parameters.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The performance of a Prussian blue cathode is affected by its structure and stability.  Through the inhibitor and temp. control, the border-rich structure was obtained, which provides a good contact between electrode-electrolyte interfaces and increases the transmission path for Na+ ions.  In addn., the as-prepd. sample with rhombohedral phase demonstrated lower band gap and lower energy barrier for Na+ ions insertion.  Benefiting from the kinetics optimization, the as-prepd. electrode exhibited initial capacity of 120 mA h g-1 and maintained nearly 80% after 280 cycles at c.d. of 100 mA g-1.  This electrode also exhibited good rate performance about 60 mA h g-1 at rate of 10 C.  The structural stability is also related to the formation of the passivation layer during the charge-discharge process.  The optimized passivation layer not only protects the electrode form the adverse side reactions at high voltage but also delivers low interface impedance.  It can be inferred that the passivation layer plays a pos. role in long cycling performance.  When the as-prepd. electrode was measured in an electrolyte with FEC additive, it exhibited a high capacity retention rate of 79% after 500 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b06880"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Low Defect FeFe(CN)6 Framework as Stable Host Material for High Performance Li-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors reported a rhombohedral Na-rich nickel hexacyanoferrate (r-NiHCF) with high discharge voltage, which also possesses long cycle stability and excellent rate capability when serving as the cathode material of Na-ion batteries.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">31</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">145-148</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ECCMF9</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1388-2481</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">An aq. rechargable Na-ion battery is developed by use of Na-deficient NaTi2(PO4)3 anode, Na-rich Na2NiFe(CN)6 cathode and aq. Na2SO4 electrolyte.  This battery system can give an av. output voltage of 1.27 V, a energy d. of 42.5 Wh kg-1 and can retain 88% of initial capacity for 250 cycles cycled at the 5 C rate.  Moreover, this aq. Na-ion battery has the advantages of low cost, environmentally friendliness and inherent safety, particularly attractive for grid-scale energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref41" class="rightTabRefNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit41" data-doi="10.1039/C4CC05830E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, X. Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, Y. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Z. F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structure optimization of Prussian blue analogue cathode materials for advanced sodium ion batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Commun.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>50</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">13377</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">13380</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C4CC05830E</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structure optimization of Prussian blue analogue cathode materials for advanced sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Dezhi; Xu, Jing; Liao, Xiao-Zhen; He, Yu-Shi; Liu, Haimei; Ma, Zi-Feng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Communications (Cambridge, United Kingdom)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">50</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">87</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">13377-13380</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHCOFS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1359-7345</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A structure optimized Prussian blue analog Na1.76Ni0.12Mn0.88[Fe(CN)6]0.98 (PBMN) is synthesized and investigated.  Coexistence of inactive Ni2+ (Fe-C≡N-Ni group) with active Mn2+/3+ (Fe-C≡N-Mn group) balances the structural disturbances caused by the redox reactions.  This cathode material exhibits particularly excellent cycle life with high capacity (118.2 mA h g-1).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref42" class="rightTabRefNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit42" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201604007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, D. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">McDonagh, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, S. Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G. X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Capacity Aqueous Potassium-Ion Batteries for Large-Scale Energy Storage</span>. <i>Adv. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>29</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1604007</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adma.201604007</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="

        <script nonce="93188a180b2354b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="93188a180b2354b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="93188a180b2354b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage =
          "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();
          });

          // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
          // $(document).click(function(event) {
          // 	event.stopPropagation();
          //   console.log(event.target);
          //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
          //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
          //     $(this).off();
          //   }
          // });
          $("#gdpr-con-btn").click(function () {
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            hideForm();
          });
        }, time);
      }

      //Show the message
      function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

    $(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
  </script>
</p>

var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
